# Tableau Essential Training

> Curt Frye dives into the power of Tableau with this in-depth course crafted to elevate your data visualization capabilities. Explore ways to connect, clean, and prepare data from various sources. Learn how to construct compelling visualizations, from basic plots to advanced maps and dashboards, tailored for impactful presentation and decision-making. Discover how to implement calculated fields, ut

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026) | 4h 43m | Intermediate | 26K learners

>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/install-tableau-drivers?u=76281980&t=90)** There are also two built in the sample data sources that you can use and those are Superstore and World Indicators.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/install-tableau-drivers?u=76281980&t=98)** I'll click Superstore and here, I have opened it and over on the left, I have Customer Name, Customer Location, Order Date, and other information.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/install-tableau-drivers?u=76281980&t=109)** So, as you can see, you can connect to just about any type of data and if you don't find a connection that you need, you can probably go to the [Tableau.com](https://Tableau.com) site and search in the Help files for a connector or the publisher of the data source that you're working with might be able to help you as well.

#### [Introduce Tableau file types](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [[Tableau]] looks like a reasonably simple program, but there is actually a lot going on.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=6)** And one of the ways that you can use Tableau to its full extent is to take advantage of different file types.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=12)** In this movie, I will describe the different file types that are available to you.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=16)** First, there is a Tableau Workbook with a .twb extension.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=22)** A Tableau workbook, which is the type of file that we will use throughout this course, stores a visualization without source data.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=30)** So if you send the workbook to someone without including the data source, such as an [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]] file, they will have limited use of that file.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=40)** A Tableau data source, on the other hand, stores the server address, password, and other information required to access a data source.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=49)** A Tableau data source has a .tds extension, and it contains the information that I have listed here.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=57)** That's mostly used for servers, and we don't really cover those in this course.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=62)** You can also create a Tableau bookmark with a .tbm extension.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=67)** A .tbm file stores a connection to a worksheet in another Tableau workbook.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=73)** So a bookmark is a great way to connect to another Tableau workbook.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=78)** There is also a Tableau data extract with a .tde extension, and this store Tableau data as a filtered and aggregated extract.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=88)** So a Tableau workbook would contain a connection to a data source, whereas a data extract would include a subsection of that data as part of the .tde file.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=101)** And finally, there is the Tableau packaged workbook.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=106)** A packaged workbook has a .twbx extension and it stores extracted data and visualization for viewing in any full version of Tableau and also Tableau Reader, which is available for free.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/introduce-tableau-file-types?u=76281980&t=120)** So if you want to include all of your data in a Tableau workbook and send it to someone you trust, you can do that in a Tableau packaged workbook.

#### [Change the summary operation](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/change-the-summary-operation?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/change-the-summary-operation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you visualize data in [[Tableau]], most of the time you will want to find the sum of whatever data you have collected.

>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=9)** In this movie, I'll describe some of the more frequently used operators and built-in functions that will save you time and help you gather important information about your business.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=18)** I'll start with mathematical operators, and the most common ones are plus for addition.

> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=25)** That can also be used for dates.

>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=53)** Other operators that you might not use as often are the percent sign for modular division, which returns the remainder of an operation.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=62)** So 8 divided by 3 would leave a remainder or modulus of 2, because 3 times 2 is 6, and then 8 minus 6 would leave you a remainder of 2.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=75)** The caret is used for exponents, so 2 caret 3 would be 2 to the third, or 8.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=82)** Then the usual comparison operators, we have equal, greater than, less than, greater than or equal to, less than or equal to, and finally not equal to.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=92)** And in Tableau, you use a less than sign followed by a greater than sign for not equal to.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=99)** That's different from other programming languages, so I thought I would mention it here.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=104)** You also have the AND, OR, and NOT logical operators that allows you to combine criteria in interesting ways.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=114)** Two or more things could all be true, that would be AND.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=117)** At least one thing could be true, that would be OR, and something would not be true, and of course that would be NOT.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=124)** Next, here are some of the commonly used functions.
>

>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=145)** Those calculations are very useful when you are looking at the range of values in a dataset.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=151)** Standard deviation finds the standard deviation of a set of values, and again, is very useful when you are doing [[Statistical Analysis]].
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=160)** Other functions that you might use are ABS, which returns the absolute value, which is the distance from zero of a number.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=168)** So 3 and minus 3 would both have an absolute value of 3.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=173)** CEILING rounds the number up to the next integer, so 20.1 would be rounded up to 21.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=180)** FLOOR rounds a number down, so 20.1 would be rounded down to 20, as would 20.9.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=188)** And finally, you have ROUND, this rounds a number to a specified number of digits.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=194)** If you rounded a number to the right of the decimal point by two digits, then you would get a two digit answer regardless of the size of the starting value.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=205)** And similarly, if you rounded it to zero, then you would round to the nearest whole number.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/present-tableau-operators-and-built-in-functions?u=76281980&t=211)** There are many other functions that you can use, but these are the ones that I wanted to focus on here.

#### [Create a calculated field](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-calculated-field?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-calculated-field?u=76281980&t=52)** To create a calculated field, go to the Analysis menu.

> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-a-table-calculation?u=76281980&t=275)** If you want to remove a table calculation, you can go to the pill on the Marks card, click it's down arrow, and then click Clear Table Calculation, and you are back to your original data.

> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/use-level-of-detail-expressions?u=76281980&t=7)** Most calculations happen at a specific level such as the sheet level, the table row level, and so on.

#### [Count occurrences and distinct items](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Speaker] Large data collections tended to contain a lot of repeated values.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=4)** For example, if you order [[Microsoft Products|products]] to help a hotel chain run, then you might see a lot of orders for items such as lamps, bedspreads, and so on.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=15)** In this movie, I will show you how to count the number of orders placed within several categories and then also show you how to count the number of distinct items in this case products that were ordered from within each category.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=30)** My sample file is 0502 count, and you can find it in the chapter five folder of the Exercise files collection.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=38)** In this workbook, I have a text table and I have already added product categories to the Row Shelf.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=46)** If I want to count the number of orders within each of those categories, then I go to the sidebar, and in the measures area, there is an automatically generated field that counts the number of records in the order details table.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=63)** And furthermore, because I have product category in the row area or on the row shelf, then what will happen when I move order details count to the data area is that we will get a count of the number of orders within each of those categories.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=80)** So I will drag order details for count, and I see that I have over 1,300 orders for furnishings, about 1000 for housekeeping and the other numbers you see there.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=94)** So that is the number of orders, but now the question might be how many different items did we order within each category?
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=103)** And for that, we need to use a different function where we count the number of distinct entries within a separate category.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=110)** So I'll drag count of order details off of the Marx card.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=115)** Now I want to determine the number of different products for which we had at least one order.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=120)** So I'll drag product name to the data area.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=125)** And what happens is that because I had a measure, [[Tableau]] thinks that I want to add it to the row shelf as a way of organizing the visualization that I'll create.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=135)** And so, it did that.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=136)** So I have text values here.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=139)** Instead, what I want to do is change it to a measure.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=143)** So I will go up to the row shelf and then click the product name pills down arrow.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=149)** And then rather than working with this data as a dimension, I'll point to measure and click the last of the functions available, which is count distinct.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=161)** This gives me a bar chart.
>

>

>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=170)** Actually, I'll click Show me again.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=172)** And I see that we have orders for 26 different products and furnishings, 19 in housekeeping, 15 in maintenance, 13 in [[Microsoft Office|office]] supplies, and 21 for items that are used in the public areas.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=184)** So remember, if you want to count the number of orders within a specific category, in this case product category, use order details count.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/count-occurrences-and-distinct-items?u=76281980&t=195)** If you want to count the number of distinct items within, in this case product categories, you would use the values that are within that, in this case product name, each one of which is assigned to a product category, and then use count distinct to create the measure that you want.

#### [Display the Summary Card](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/display-the-summary-card?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/display-the-summary-card?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] You can summarize your [[Tableau]] data in many different ways, but most formulas only provide one value at a time.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/display-the-summary-card?u=76281980&t=7)** If you want to display several summaries of your data, such as minimums, maximums, and standard deviations, you can display the Summary card.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/display-the-summary-card?u=76281980&t=16)** And in this movie I will show you how to do that, and also how to change the values that appear on that card.

>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/display-the-summary-card?u=76281980&t=82)** You might not have encountered the median before, which is 253.5, and that might seem odd because there are no values that have any decimals as part of the data list.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/display-the-summary-card?u=76281980&t=97)** What happened was that Tableau looked at the data and internally sorted these values into either ascending or descending order, and then calculated the middle value.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/display-the-summary-card?u=76281980&t=109)** However, because we have an even number, which is 20, then if the two middle values, basically the 10th and 11th, are different, then Tableau takes the average.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/display-the-summary-card?u=76281980&t=119)** So let me go ahead and sort these values into ascending order.

>

>

>

>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/display-the-summary-card?u=76281980&t=175)** You can change the summary operations that appear on the Summary card by going over to the card itself, moving the mouse pointer over the title bar, which is next to the title, and then clicking its down arrow.

> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/display-the-summary-card?u=76281980&t=230)** And now your visualization takes up the entirety of the window.

> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=131)** And I can have an automatic forecast length, which is for the next four months, or I can go further into the future.

> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=139)** But of course, as we go further into the future, the predictions or the forecast will become less accurate.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=146)** But let's see what it looks like.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=148)** So instead of Automatic for the next four months, I'll click Exactly, and I have one year.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=155)** So we can see that the forecast goes down significantly.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=158)** So let's just take it back to two quarters.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=164)** So there I have it.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=165)** I have the first forecast that goes up a bit, and then the forecast goes down.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=170)** And that, if I click OK, is based on my previous data.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=176)** And again, the farther you go into the future, the less accurate these forecasts will be, and you should use your business knowledge, in this case, about your maintenance cost, to provide a different kind of estimate.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=188)** But the forecast is a great place to start.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/forecast-future-values?u=76281980&t=192)** When you're done, you can right-click it, point to Forecast, and then click Show Forecast, which currently has a check mark next to it, and then, when you click, the forecast is removed.

>

> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-nested-sort?u=76281980&t=102)** But let me show you what happens if I want to sort by a field not currently in the visualization.

> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-nested-sort?u=76281980&t=174)** But if you know that's the case, then you can have a visualization with the total value of the orders for each category and have a sense of the quantity that was ordered within each category.

#### [Create a wildcard filter](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] A lot of business data follows patterns, both in the sense that the data reflects daily, monthly, or quarterly trends, or that it contains similar values for related items.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=11)** For example, product names that you might buy for a hotel could have towel, T-O-W-E-L, in the name.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=18)** In this movie, I will show you how to create a wild card filter by searching for items within an item list.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=26)** My sample file is 06_04_Wildcard, and you can find it in the chapter six folder of the exercise files collection.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=34)** In this workbook, I have a text table with [[Microsoft Products|products]] that I buy from my hotels, broken down by product category and then by product name.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=44)** And if I scroll down, you can see that I have five different categories and quite a few items within them.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=55)** I can search within this list by creating what's called a wild card filter.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=60)** I will filter by product name, so I'll drag that field to the filters card.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=66)** And the filters dialogue box appears.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=69)** And I'll drag that to the right, so we can see the data in the background.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=73)** And in the dialogue box, I'll click the "wild card" tab.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=78)** And here, I can search for values based on a number of different techniques.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=84)** And those are "contains," "starts with," "ends with," and "exactly matches."
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=90)** I won't use "exactly matches" because that is essentially a single item search.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=95)** If I wanted to search for an iron, then I would search for "exactly that."
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=102)** And that's not what I want to do.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=104)** So instead, I will do "contains."
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=107)** And here, [[Tableau]] will look for any product name that has the characters that I type in in that order, anywhere within the product name.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=119)** So if I type "T-O-W", and click "apply," then I get the towel rack, the hand towel, and the large towel.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=129)** And I will type "towel" the whole way out and apply it, so you can see that it doesn't actually change the results.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=138)** If I were to select the "starts with" radio button, and click "apply," then I only get the towel rack.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=146)** Because even though the other two hand towels and bath towels had "towel," they do not start with that string.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=154)** If I were to select the "ends with" radio button, and click "apply," then the opposite happens.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=160)** I have the hand towel, and the large towel, but not the towel rack.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=165)** I can also reverse the filter by excluding just the items that are displayed by the filter.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=172)** So, if I go back to "contains," and click "apply," then I have towel rack, hand towel, large towel.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=180)** If I check the "exclude" box, then it changes to "does not contain," "does not start with," then click "apply."
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=186)** And I have basically everything except for those three items that I had displayed earlier.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=193)** So, that is how a wild card filter works.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-wildcard-filter?u=76281980&t=196)** You can click "clear" to clear the filter, and then click "okay," and go back to your work.

#### [Filter data using parameters](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you create a filter, [[Tableau]] limits the visualization summary to the values you want.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=6)** You can always edit the filter, but you could also use a visible control to make changes.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=12)** That visible control would be based on a parameter.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=16)** In this movie, I will show you how to define a parameter and use it as a filter.
>

>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=31)** In this workbook, I have a single worksheet, and on it, I have the amount spent for all of the [[Microsoft Products|products]] that I've ordered for my company, and let's say that I want to show the top one to 10, and I want to be able to control how many are being shown.

>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=68)** The create parameter dialog box appears, and I could change the name if I wanted.

>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=76)** The data type is integer.
>

>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=83)** There are other formats available, including floating point numbers, which could be used if you wanted to set, for example, a currency value that included cents, so any element less than one.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=98)** I'll stay with integer, and I won't change the display format, but I do have a set of items that I could pick from.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=108)** I'll leave the current value at three for the moment, and I'll also use the current value when the workbook opens.

> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=115)** I want to allow a range of values, so I will leave range selected, and the range of values I'm going to change, the minimum is currently three, because that is the lowest amount for an order, but what I actually want is to have a range from one to 10 values.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=134)** So I have one, and then for the maximum, I'll have 10, and this will remain fixed.

> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/filter-data-using-parameters?u=76281980&t=162)** Everything looks good, so I will click okay, and I have a new area over in the sidebar where the parameter was created.
>

#### [Define a set](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you analyze a data source, you will often find that certain values go together.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=6)** For example, you might treat a set of states in the US as a region, and it would make sense to define a set of those values.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=15)** In this movie, I will show you how to define a set, which you can use to analyze a portion of your data.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=22)** My sample file is 07_05_CreateSet, and you can find it i the chapter seven folder of the exercise files collection.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=30)** There are several ways to define a set, the way that I like to do it is to create a visualization and then select my values from there.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=40)** I'll work with the property state field, so I'll drag that to the drop field name here, and it goes onto the row shelf.

>

>

>

>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=99)** It looks like a pair of interlocking circles or a Venn diagram.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=105)** So I'll click that, click create set, and there I have it.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=110)** So, I have the name of the set, and also the three items that are in it.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=116)** If I accidentally click something that I didn't want to include, then I could click the remove button, which looks like a red X, to get rid of it.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=126)** However, in this case, everything is the way I want.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=130)** The name is highlighted, so I will change that, and I will call that West Coast States, and click okay.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=141)** I'll click away from the visualization.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=143)** And over on the left, I see that I have my new set named West Coast States, and I can tell it's a set because to the left of the field's pill, I have the ring or Venn diagram icon indicating that I have a set.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=161)** One way to use a set is to create a filter based on it.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=165)** So if I drag West Coast states to the Filters card, then I have only the states that are included in that set.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=176)** And if I drag it away, then I go back to my entire dataset.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=182)** Sets are extremely useful, and they are distinct from groups in one important way.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=189)** When you define a set, you have different values that are related in some way.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=194)** In this case, they're states on the west coast of the United States, whereas a group is usually thought of as the same value, just with different names.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-set?u=76281980&t=204)** So groups help you clean up your data, whereas sets help you analyze your data.

>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-pie-charts?u=76281980&t=160)** So, instead, I will press Control-Z to move back, Control-Z again to undo, and I am back with my 5-category pie chart, which I think provides a terrific summary of my data.

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-scatter-plots?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In many cases, you will collect data with two numerical attributes, which you can then graph using a scatter plot.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-scatter-plots?u=76281980&t=8)** For example, if you track customer income versus the amount that they spend, which would be their sales total, you can create a plot or graph that will visualize that relationship.

>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-scatter-plots?u=76281980&t=35)** I have data about my customer's income and then also the amount that they spend.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-scatter-plots?u=76281980&t=42)** If I want to see how those two relate, then I can add both of those measures to my visualization.

>

>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-scatter-plots?u=76281980&t=79)** So I have total income of 3,734 and sales total of 20,690.

>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-scatter-plots?u=76281980&t=157)** If you want to change the shapes and also their size, you can go to the marks card.

> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-scatter-plots?u=76281980&t=191)** And then you can make the marks smaller, which actually looks pretty good for this data set.

>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-scatter-plots?u=76281980&t=213)** When you look at the scatter plot, you can get some information about your data.

> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-scatter-plots?u=76281980&t=248)** So those are just some of the things that you can determine by looking at your data.

> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-scatter-plots?u=76281980&t=253)** You can look for trends, you can look for groups of data, and you can also perhaps find individual customers that you would like to work with more.

#### [Summarize data using a smart histogram](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/summarize-data-using-a-smart-histogram?u=76281980)

#### [Create a treemap](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-treemap?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-treemap?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Treemaps display values as they contribute to a whole.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-treemap?u=76281980&t=5)** Like a pie chart, a treemap uses the size of the area devoted to an input to indicate its contribution.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-treemap?u=76281980&t=11)** Unlike a pie chart though, a treemap also uses shades of color to indicate value.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-treemap?u=76281980&t=17)** Darker shades generally mean larger values and lighter shades mean smaller, and even negative values can be indicated using treemap colors.

> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-highlight-table?u=76281980&t=140)** So I have orange and blue, so here I have orange as the lowest values and blue is the highest.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-highlight-table?u=76281980&t=146)** However, a lot of designers don't like to use two different colors in a diverging palette like this because it can be hard to remember that a darker brick red, essentially color here, is small, at least just by looking at it before you see the numbers, and remember that a dark blue is largest, so they tend to focus mostly on single colors.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-highlight-table?u=76281980&t=170)** And you have quite a few of them available to you.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-highlight-table?u=76281980&t=173)** One last thing to think about is that if you work with individuals who don't see colors well, then you might want to just use gray.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-highlight-table?u=76281980&t=182)** So use gray, click Apply, and here you have lighter gray colors that are perfect for the lower numbers and a darker gray, getting sort of close to black, for the larger numbers.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/create-a-highlight-table?u=76281980&t=195)** And when you're done, you can't click OK, click away and continue your work.

>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/format-chart-elements?u=76281980&t=289)** At a drop line, we'll draw a line from a specific point and drop it to both the X or horizontal axis and the Y or vertical axis.
>

>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/format-chart-elements?u=76281980&t=313)** It has been added for this point, and that gives me a better way to see what the value is on both of my axes.
>

>

>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-a-trend-line-to-a-chart?u=76281980&t=84)** So the next question is, how well does this line actually explain the data?
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-a-trend-line-to-a-chart?u=76281980&t=89)** To get that information, you can move the mouse pointer over the trend line itself, and a tool tip appears that displays three pieces of information.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-a-trend-line-to-a-chart?u=76281980&t=99)** The first is the actual equation that is used to generate the line.
>

>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-a-trend-line-to-a-chart?u=76281980&t=107)** And R squared tells you how much of the actual data is explained by the equation that generates a trend line.
>

>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-a-trend-line-to-a-chart?u=76281980&t=119)** So that means that unfortunately, this model isn't very useful.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-a-trend-line-to-a-chart?u=76281980&t=124)** That conclusion is reinforced by the P value of 0.28.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-a-trend-line-to-a-chart?u=76281980&t=129)** And that might seem like a fairly low value, but in fact, the usual cutoff for statistical significance is 0.05.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-a-trend-line-to-a-chart?u=76281980&t=138)** So unfortunately, our P value is much too high.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-a-trend-line-to-a-chart?u=76281980&t=140)** So even though the trend is overall down, unfortunately just using mathematics doesn't give us a good indication of what the actual trend will be in the future.

> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=144)** So from here it's fairly easy to see which months had above average order amounts and which ones had below average.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=152)** If I want to remove this reference line, then I can right click the vertical axis again, and I can either edit the reference line, which opens the dialogue box that we just had, or click Remove Reference Line, and I go back to the original.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=168)** Another way to analyze your data and visualize it is to use quantiles.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=173)** Quantiles separate your data into segments.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=177)** If you had quartiles, which would mean four segments, then you would have the bottom 25%, then the next 25%, and we are talking in sum of order total, then the third 25%, and then the top 25%.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=191)** So let's see what that looks like by adding quartiles to this visualization.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=197)** I'll right click the vertical axis, and I'll click Add Reference Line.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=202)** And then in the Add Reference Line dialogue box, I will click Distribution.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=207)** And for the computation of the value, click the down arrow, and I'll say Quantiles.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=214)** And the number of tiles will be four.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=216)** Although, I could make it three.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=218)** I could make it five. Any number.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=220)** However, too few means you don't get enough data or enough information about your data, and too many can be very confusing.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=228)** So we'll stay with four.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=230)** I'll click away, and everything, it looks good.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=232)** I can see it in the background.
>

>

>

>

>

>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/add-reference-lines-and-quartile-indicators?u=76281980&t=309)** When you're done working with the reference line, in this case, the quartile visualization, you can right click the axis again, click Remove Reference Line, and you are back to your original data.

>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/enhance-map-analysis-using-distance-measurement-tools?u=76281980&t=197)** But again, if I scroll in, probably about to here and I drag again, then I do get the measurement.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/enhance-map-analysis-using-distance-measurement-tools?u=76281980&t=205)** So if you are looking at your data and trying to use the radial tool to estimate distances, if you don't see them, it is likely that you're zoomed out too far.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-filter-action?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [[Dashboards]] provide useful summaries for your company's data, bringing the most important values to one place.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-filter-action?u=76281980&t=7)** In many cases, you will want to look at a subset of your data.

> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-filter-action?u=76281980&t=170)** If you want to remove the filter, then you can either click away within the control itself.

>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-highlight-action?u=76281980&t=182)** So I have category highlight, run on select, the source is Dashboard 1, and it works on all fields.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/tableau-essential-training-29553026/define-a-highlight-action?u=76281980&t=188)** So I'll click okay.
>

>

>
